Defining Eloquent Simile (al-Tashbih al-Baligh)
Author defines baligh tashbih with analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an eloquent simile by recognizing the omission of the tool and the aspect of similarity.

Core Idea
Eloquent simile omits both tool and aspect, leaving only the two compared terms.

In Arabic rhetoric, similes are classified by their explicitness. The most compact form is the eloquent simile (al-tashbih al-baligh), where the word 'like' and the shared attribute are dropped. This lesson unpacks the definition using the author's vivid examples of deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of a full simile
A full simile has three parts: the subject (mushtabbah), object (mushtabbah bihi), tool of simile (adaat), and aspect of similarity (wajh al-shabah).
2
Recognize what is omitted in baligh tashbih
The definition states that both the tool (e.g., ك) and the aspect are omitted. Only the subject and object remain.
3
Understand the analogy of the tailless animal
The author compares the omission to an animal lacking a tail (abtar), fingers (ajdham), or hand (aqta'). The meaning is like a deficient body, emphasizing the loss of essential parts.
4
Note the nature of the aspect of similarity
The aspect is absolute deficiency, but it is sensory in the object (e.g., a tailless animal) and moral in the subject (e.g., a deficient act). This distinction is crucial for correct identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالكَلَامُ عَلَى كُلِّ : مِنْ بَابِ التَّشْبِيهِ البَلِيغِ - وَهُوَ : «مَا حُذِفَ فِيهِ أَدَاةُ التَّشْبِيهِ وَوَجْهُ الشَّبَهِ ، وَالمَعْنَى : فَهُوَ كَالأَبْتَرِ» - الَّذِي هُوَ : مَقْطُوعُ الذَّنَبِ ، أَوْ كَالأَجْذَمِ» - الَّذِي هُوَ مَنْ ذَهَبَتْ أَنَامِلُهُ مِنَ الجُذَامِ ، - أَوْ كَالأَقْطَع» - الَّذِي هُوَ : مَقْطُوعُ اليَدِ ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَوَجْهُ الشَّبَهِ : مُطْلَقُ النَّقْصِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ فِي المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ حِسّيًّا، وَفِي المُشَبَّهِ مَعْنَوِيًّا . أَوْ مِنْ بَابِ الاِسْتِعَارَةِ المُصَرَّحَةِ عَلَى الخِلافِ بَيْنَ الجُمْهُورِ وَالسَّعْدِ فِي نَحْوِ : زَيْدُ أَسَدٌ»؛ حَيْثُ قَالَ الجُمْهُورُ : يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مِنْ بَابِ التَّشْبِيهِ البَلِيغْ، وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مِنْ بَابِ الاسْتِعَارَةِ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا يُجْمَعُ فِيهَا بَيْنَ طَرَفَيِ التَّشْبِيهِ - أَعْني : المُشَبَّهَ وَالمُشَبَّهَ بِهِ . وَجَوَّزَ السَّعْدُ ذَلِكَ، وَمَنَعَ لُزُومِ الجَمْعِ بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ; بِجَعْلِهِ المُشَبَّةَ : «الرَّجُلُ الشُّجَاعُ»، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ مَذْكُورٍ فِي التَّرْكِيبِ ، وَالمَذْكُورُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فَرْدٌ مِنْهُ وَهُوَ زَيْدٌ.
And speech about every [case] is from the category of the eloquent simile—which is: 'that in which the tool of simile and the aspect of similarity are omitted, and the meaning: it is like the tailless [animal]'—which is: cut off of the tail, or like the mangy—who is one whose fingers have gone from leprosy, or like the amputee—who is: one with a cut hand. And in every case, the aspect of similarity is absolute deficiency, although it is sensory in the object of comparison and moral in the subject. Or from the category of explicit metaphor, according to the disagreement between the majority and al-Sa'd in cases like 'Zayd is a lion'; where the majority said: it must be from the category of the eloquent simile, and it is not permissible to be from the category of metaphor, because in metaphor the two sides of the simile—I mean the subject and the object of comparison—are not combined. And al-Sa'd permitted that, and denied the necessity of combining the two sides, by making the object of comparison 'the brave man', which is not mentioned in the construction, and what is mentioned is only an individual of it, and that is Zayd.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and dialectical commentary on the rhetorical classification of phrases like 'Zayd is a lion'. The author first defines the eloquent simile (al-tashbih al-baligh) as that in which both the tool of simile (like 'ka') and the aspect of similarity (wajh al-shabah) are omitted. The meaning is like the tailless animal—each example (abtar, ajdham, aqta') represents a different form of bodily deficiency, but the shared aspect of similarity is absolute deficiency. In the simile, the deficiency is sensory in the object (e.g., a tail-less animal) but moral in the subject (e.g., the act being described as deficient). Then the author presents a disagreement: when we say 'Zayd is a lion', the majority hold it must be an eloquent simile (not a metaphor) because a metaphor does not combine both the subject and object of comparison explicitly. Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) disagrees: he permits it to be a metaphor by positing the object of comparison as 'the brave man' (the universal), which is not explicitly mentioned in the phrase—only an individual (Zayd) is mentioned, and that individual is a specific instance of that universal. Thus al-Sa'd argues that the two sides of the simile are not directly combined, so it can be a metaphor.

Key Terms
التشبيه البليغ
أداة التشبيه
وجه الشبه
الأبتر
الأجذم
الأقطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is omitted in an eloquent simile (al-tashbih al-baligh)?
A) The subject only.
B) The tool of simile and the aspect of similarity.
C) The object of comparison only.
D) Both the subject and the object.

Key Takeaway
Eloquent simile is defined by the omission of the tool and the aspect, leaving only the two compared terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the Hadith 'المؤمن مرآة أخيه' (The believer is the mirror of his brother) meets the definition of eloquent simile. Explain the omission of tool and aspect.

The Dispute over 'Zayd is a Lion': Simile or Metaphor?
Author presents the disagreement between the majority and al-Sa'd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the majority's argument that 'Zayd is a lion' is an eloquent simile and al-Sa'd's argument that it can be a metaphor.

Core Idea
The disagreement hinges on whether a metaphor can explicitly combine both the subject and object of comparison.

In Arabic rhetoric, the phrase 'Zayd is a lion' appears straightforward, but scholars debated its classification. The majority insisted it must be an eloquent simile because metaphor cannot combine both sides explicitly. Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) disagreed, arguing that the real comparison is to 'the brave man', not 'lion'. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase under dispute
The phrase 'زيد أسد' (Zayd is a lion) explicitly states both a subject (Zayd) and an object (lion).
2
State the majority position
The majority (جمهور) argue that it must be an eloquent simile (tashbih baligh) because metaphor does not combine both the subject and object of comparison explicitly.
They define metaphor as not having both sides mentioned together.
3
State al-Sa'd's position
Al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) permits it to be a metaphor by positing the object of comparison as 'the brave man' (الرجل الشجاع), which is not mentioned explicitly. The mentioned 'lion' is an individual instance of that universal.
Thus, al-Sa'd denies the necessity of combining the two sides literally.
4
Compare the two views
The majority see 'lion' as the direct object; al-Sa'd sees it as a representation of the attribute. This shows how the same phrase can be analyzed differently based on underlying assumptions about what constitutes the object of comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالكَلَامُ عَلَى كُلِّ : مِنْ بَابِ التَّشْبِيهِ البَلِيغِ - وَهُوَ : «مَا حُذِفَ فِيهِ أَدَاةُ التَّشْبِيهِ وَوَجْهُ الشَّبَهِ ، وَالمَعْنَى : فَهُوَ كَالأَبْتَرِ» - الَّذِي هُوَ : مَقْطُوعُ الذَّنَبِ ، أَوْ كَالأَجْذَمِ» - الَّذِي هُوَ مَنْ ذَهَبَتْ أَنَامِلُهُ مِنَ الجُذَامِ ، - أَوْ كَالأَقْطَع» - الَّذِي هُوَ : مَقْطُوعُ اليَدِ ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ فَوَجْهُ الشَّبَهِ : مُطْلَقُ النَّقْصِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ فِي المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ حِسّيًّا، وَفِي المُشَبَّهِ مَعْنَوِيًّا . أَوْ مِنْ بَابِ الاِسْتِعَارَةِ المُصَرَّحَةِ عَلَى الخِلافِ بَيْنَ الجُمْهُورِ وَالسَّعْدِ فِي نَحْوِ : زَيْدُ أَسَدٌ»؛ حَيْثُ قَالَ الجُمْهُورُ : يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مِنْ بَابِ التَّشْبِيهِ البَلِيغْ، وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مِنْ بَابِ الاسْتِعَارَةِ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا يُجْمَعُ فِيهَا بَيْنَ طَرَفَيِ التَّشْبِيهِ - أَعْني : المُشَبَّهَ وَالمُشَبَّهَ بِهِ . وَجَوَّزَ السَّعْدُ ذَلِكَ، وَمَنَعَ لُزُومِ الجَمْعِ بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ; بِجَعْلِهِ المُشَبَّةَ : «الرَّجُلُ الشُّجَاعُ»، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ مَذْكُورٍ فِي التَّرْكِيبِ ، وَالمَذْكُورُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فَرْدٌ مِنْهُ وَهُوَ زَيْدٌ.

Checkpoint
According to the majority (الجمهور), why can 'Zayd is a lion' not be considered a metaphor?
A) Because a metaphor requires the tool of simile.
B) Because a metaphor combines both the subject and object explicitly.
C) Because a metaphor does not combine both sides of the simile explicitly.

The classification of 'Zayd is a lion' as simile or metaphor depends on whether one considers the literal object (lion) or a conceptual object (brave man) as the true basis of comparison.

Definition of Idafi Beginning and Resolving Apparent Conflict
Al-Bajuri defines idafi beginning and lists ways to reconcile basmala and hamdala hadiths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqi and idafi beginnings and apply al-Bajuri's methods to reconcile conflicting hadiths.

Core Idea
The two hadiths on basmala and hamdala are not contradictory; they apply to different types of beginning.

In this lesson, we examine al-Bajuri's definition of relational beginning (al-ibtida' al-idafi) and see how it resolves an apparent conflict between two hadiths—one recommending basmala and the other hamdala. By understanding these concepts, you will learn how scholars reconcile seemingly contradictory texts using careful classification and additional principles from usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of beginnings
Recognize that al-Bajuri distinguishes between al-ibtida' al-haqiqi (true beginning) and al-ibtida' al-idafi (relational beginning). The hadith on basmala is applied to the first, and hamdala to the second.
2
Extract the definition of idafi beginning
From the text: 'al-ibtida' al-idafi is the beginning with what precedes the purpose, even if something preceded it.' Note the key differentia: it is relative to what follows.
The genus is 'beginning', the differentia is 'with what precedes the purpose'.
3
Apply the distinction to the hadiths
The basmala is the true beginning (haqiqi) because nothing in the discourse precedes it. The hamdala is relational (idafi) because it comes after the basmala, even though it precedes the main purpose. This assignment follows the Qur'an and consensus.
4
Recognize additional reconciliation methods
Al-Bajuri lists three more ways to remove contradiction: (1) beginning is a continuum from composition to purpose, (2) the basmala hadith is more authentic (asahh), (3) if a mutlaq text exists, it governs; otherwise the muqayyad is carried on the mutlaq.
These are supplementary to the primary classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِضَافِيٌّ، وَهُوَ : الابْتِدَاء بِمَا تَقَدَّمَ أَمَامَ المَقْصُودِ، وَإِنْ سَبَقَهُ شَيْءٌ. فَحُمِلَ حَدِيثُ البَسْمَلَةِ عَلَى النَّوْعِ الأَوَّلِ، وَحَدِيثُ الحَمْدَلَةِ عَلَى الثَّانِي، وَلَمْ يُعْكَسْ؛ تَأسياً بِالكِتَابِ العَزِيزِ، وَعَمَلاً بِالإِجْمَاعِ . وَبَقِيَ لِدَفْعِ التَّعَارُضِ أَوْجُهُ أَخَرُ : مِنْهَا : أَنَّ الاِبْتَدَاءَ أَمْرٌ مُمْتَدٌ مِنَ الأَخْذِ فِي التَّألِيفِ إِلَى الشُّرُوع فِي المَقْصُودِ. وَمِنْهَا : أَنَّ شَرْطَ التَّعَارُضِ تَسَاوِي الحَدِيثَيْنِ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ هُنَا؛ لِأَنَّ حَدِيثَ البَسْمَلَةِ أَصَحُ . وَمِنْهَا : أَنَّ مَحَلَّ التَّعَارُضِ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ هُنَاكَ مُطْلَقٌ كَمَا هُنَا، فَإِنَّهُ وَرَدَ: كُلُّ أَمْرٍ ذِي بَالٍ لَا يُبْدَأُ فِيهِ بِذِكْرِ اللهِ ... إلخ، وَإِلَّا حُمِلَ المُقَيَّدُ عَلَى المُطْلَقِ.
And the relational (beginning): it is the beginning with what precedes before the purpose, even if something preceded it. So the hadith of basmala is carried on the first type, and the hadith of hamdala on the second, and not reversed; following the Noble Book and acting on consensus. And there remain other ways to remove the contradiction: among them: that the beginning is an extended matter from taking up composition to starting the purpose. And among them: that the condition for contradiction is equality of the two hadiths, and it is not so here, because the hadith of basmala is more authentic. And among them: that the place for contradiction is when there is no unrestricted as here, for it has come: 'Every important matter that does not begin with dhikr of Allah...' etc., otherwise the restricted is carried on the unrestricted.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of beginning, al-ibtida' al-idafi (relational beginning), as starting with something that precedes the main purpose, even if that something itself is preceded by something else. The author then explains how two seemingly contradictory hadiths—one recommending starting with basmala and the other with hamdala—are reconciled. The basmala hadith is taken as the true initial beginning (haqiqi), while the hamdala hadith is considered a relational beginning (idafi) because it is preceded by the basmala. This application follows the Qur'anic practice and scholarly consensus. The author then lists three additional ways to resolve any perceived contradiction. First, the 'beginning' can be understood as a continuous period from the start of composing to the commencement of the main subject, allowing both to be valid at different phases. Second, the condition for actual contradiction is that both hadiths be of equal authenticity; here, the basmala hadith is considered stronger (asahh), so it takes precedence. Third, if there is an unrestricted text (mutlaq)—like the hadith 'Every important matter not beginning with dhikr Allah is incomplete'—then the unrestricted governs, and the restricted hadith (like hamdala) is carried upon it. If no unrestricted text exists, the restricted text is carried on the unrestricted, which is the opposite of the usual usul rule, but justified by the context.

Key Terms
الإبتداء الإضافي
الإبتداء الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, why is the hadith of basmala applied to the first type (haqiqi) and the hadith of hamdala to the second (idafi)?
A) Because the basmala hadith is stronger in authenticity.
B) Because the basmala is always said before hamdala in practice.
C) Because the hamdala hadith is weak and thus must be interpreted metaphorically.
D) Because the consensus of scholars dictated that division.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between haqiqi and idafi beginnings is a key tool for reconciling apparent contradictions in Islamic textual sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Suppose you encounter two hadiths: one saying 'Start with praising Allah' (hamdala) and another 'Start with His name' (basmala). Using al-Bajuri's classification, explain how these can be reconciled.
?
Task 2: Identify a real-life example from Islamic practice where the concept of relational beginning applies (e.g., beginning a speech with basmala before the actual topic).

Dialectical Refinement of Haml al-Mutlaq 'ala al-Muqayyad
Al-Bajuri presents an objection and answer in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against carrying mutlaq to muqayyad and identify the condition that restricts its application.

Core Idea
Carrying mutlaq to muqayyad is not absolute; it only holds when the restrictor is single.

In usul al-fiqh, a common rule is that when an unrestricted command (mutlaq) appears alongside a restricted one (muqayyad), the unrestricted is carried on the restricted. However, this rule has conditions. In this passage, Al-Bajuri presents an objection that uses examples from the Qur'an to challenge a previous statement, and then provides a nuanced answer that clarifies when the rule applies. Let's unpack this dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objector's Claim
The objector states that the rule among usuliyyin is to carry the unrestricted (mutlaq) to the restricted (muqayyad), citing the verses of zihar (freeing a slave) and killing (freeing a believing slave) as evidence that the unrestricted is limited by the restricted.
Note that the objector uses the opposite rule to challenge the author's earlier position.
2
Locate the Author's Answer
Al-Bajuri answers that the rule of carrying mutlaq to muqayyad is conditional on the restrictor (al-muqayyid) being only one. If there are multiple different restrictions, it cannot be applied because the restrictions conflict and arbitrary selection is prohibited.
The answer preserves the author's earlier rule by showing the objection's rule only applies in specific circumstances.
3
Assess the Validity of the Answer
Consider why the condition of a single restrictor resolves the objection: when multiple conflicting restrictions exist, carrying the mutlaq to all is impossible, and choosing one arbitrarily (tahakkum) is unacceptable. Therefore, the author's original rule (carrying muqayyad to mutlaq, i.e., restricting to the unrestricted) stands in the case of multiple restrictors.
This step practises evaluating the logical soundness of the dialectical response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قِيلَ: القَاعِدَةُ عِنْدَ الأُصُولِيِّينَ وَغَيْرِهِمْ عَكْسُ ذَلِكَ - أَعْنِي : حَمْلَ المُطْلَقِ عَلَى المُقَيَّدِ - كَمَا فِي آيَتَي الظَّهَارِ وَالقَتْلِ، فَإِنَّهُمْ حَمَلُوا المُطْلَقَةَ عَنِ التَّقِيدِ بِـ «المُؤْمِنَةِ» عَلَى الْمُقَيَّدَةِ بِهَا . أَجِيْبَ بِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ مَشْرُوطٌ بِكَوْنِ المُقَيِّدِ وَاحِداً فَقَطْ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا كَانَ مُتَعَدِّداً وَتَغَايَرَتِ(۱) القُبُودُ (۲) ، إِذْ لَا جَائِزَ أَنْ يُحْمَلَ المُطْلَقُ عَلَى الكُلِّ لِتَنَافِي القُيُودِ، وَلَا (۳) أَنْ يُحْمَلَ عَلَى وَاحِدٍ دُونَ آخَرَ؛ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَّحَكُم.
For if it is said: The rule according to the usuliyyin and others is the opposite of that—meaning carrying the unrestricted to the restricted—as in the two verses of zihar and killing, for they carried the unrestricted from being restricted by 'believing' onto the restricted by it. It is answered: That is conditional on the restrictor being only one, contrary to when it is multiple and the restrictions differ, because it is not permissible to carry the unrestricted to all due to the conflict of restrictions, nor to carry it to one to the exclusion of another due to arbitrariness.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the third way of resolving the conflict mentioned earlier, where the author said that if there is no unrestricted text, the restricted is carried on the unrestricted. The objector points out that in usul al-fiqh, the standard rule is the opposite: the unrestricted (mutlaq) is carried on the restricted (muqayyad), as seen in the examples of zihar oath and killing verses. In those verses, the word 'raqaba' (slave) was unrestricted, but it was later restricted by 'mu'mina' (believing), so the unrestricted ruling was limited to the restricted form. The answer clarifies that this rule only applies when the restrictor (al-muqayyid) is single. When there are multiple different restrictions, as in the case at hand, it is not possible to carry the unrestricted to all of them because they conflict, nor is it permissible to choose one arbitrarily (tahakkum). Therefore, the earlier statement about carrying the restricted to the unrestricted is valid in the specific context where there are multiple different restrictions.

Key Terms
المطلق
المقيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the answer, under what condition is the unrestricted carried on the restricted?
A) When the restrictions are multiple and differ from each other.
B) When the restrictor is single only.
C) When the unrestricted text is from hadith rather than Qur'an.
D) When the restricted text is more authentic.

Key Takeaway
The rule of carrying unrestricted to restricted only applies when there is a single restrictor; multiple conflicting restrictions prevent its use and justify the opposite rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a hadith: 'The Prophet forbade selling food until it is possessed' (mutlaq) and another hadith: 'Do not sell food until you measure it' (muqayyad). If there is only this one restriction, the mutlaq is carried to the muqayyad. But suppose there is a third hadith that restricts it to 'until you weigh it'—now there are two different restrictions. Explain, using the condition from this lesson, whether the rule applies.
?
Practise a dialectical exchange: Student A argues that the ruling on zakat al-fitr is mutlaq (one sa' of food) and Student B objects citing a hadith restricting it to a specific type (e.g., barley). Student A then defends by pointing out multiple conflicting restrictions. Write the dialogue following the structure of the passage.

Lam al-Istihqaq: Linking a Deserved Meaning
Al-Anbabi defines lam al-istihqaq with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify lam al-istihqaq in a sentence and distinguish it from lam al-ikhtisas and lam al-milk.

Core Idea
Lam al-istihqaq ties a deserved meaning to an essence.

In this lesson, we explore the first type of lam: lam al-istihqaq. Al-Anbabi defines it clearly: it occurs between a meaning and an essence, indicating that the meaning is deserved by the essence. This distinction is crucial for precise interpretation of Quranic verses and classical Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Define lam al-istihqaq
From the passage: 'occurring between a meaning and an essence.' This means the lam connects an abstract concept (like praise) to a concrete entity (like Allah).
2
Analyze the Quranic example: 'al-hamdu lillah'
Praise (meaning) is linked to Allah (essence), showing that praise is deserved by Allah alone. This is the first verse after the basmala.
3
Analyze the second example: 'waylun li-l-mutaffifin'
Woe (meaning) is linked to the defrauders (essence), but only if woe is understood as punishment, not a proper noun for a valley in Hell. This highlights the importance of lexical interpretation.
Al-Anbabi notes this condition explicitly.
4
Distinguish from other lam types
Lam al-ikhtisas involves two essences with no ownership; lam al-milk involves two essences with ownership. Lam al-istihqaq always involves a meaning, not a concrete entity on both sides.
This is the key differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنبابي قوله : (فَـ «اللَّامُ فِي اللهِ »: إِمَّا لِلِاسْتِحْقَاقِ ... إلخ) الفرقُ بين هذه الثلاثة : أنَّ : (۱) - لامَ الاستحقاق هي: «الواقعةُ بين معنى وذات»؛ نحو: «الحمد لله، و: ﴿وَيْلٌ لِلْمُطَفِّفِينَ﴾ (المطففين: ١)؛ بناءً على أنَّ «الوَيْلَ» اسمٌ للعذاب، لا على أنَّه اسم وادٍ في جهنَّم. (۲) - ولامَ الاختصاص هي : «الواقعةُ بين ذاتين، ومدخولها لا يملك»؛ نحو: «الجُلُّ لِلدَّابَّةِ». أو : «بين ذاتين ومصاحب مدخولها لا يملك»؛ نحو: «لِزَيْدٍ ابْنُ»؛ إذ الابنُ لا يملك، و: «أَنْتَ لِي وَأَنَا لَكَ» إذا كان كلٌّ مِنَ المخاطب والمتكلّم حرا . والرَّاجِحُ : أنَّ المراد بـ «الاختصاص» هنا : التَّعلُّق والارتباط، لا القصر. (۳) - ولام الملك هي : «الواقعةُ بین ذاتين، ومدخولها يملك»؛ نحو: «المَالُ لِزَيْدِ». وقد يعبر أيضاً عن الأول والأخير بـ: «لام الاختصاص، كما أنَّـه قـد يـعـبـر عـن الـثـانـي بـ «لام الاستحقاق» ؛ هذا حاصل ما في الأشموني وحاشية المحقق الصَّبَّان عليه وعلى هذا، فجعل اللَّام هنا للملك لا يظهر ; لأنَّ الحمد معنى لا ذات، وأجاب بعضهم : بأن جعلها هنا للملك أحد قولين، وهو أنَّه لا يشترط أن تكون بين ذاتين، وإن كان خلاف المشهور، فحرّر . وجعلها للاختصاص لا يظهر أيضاً ; لِمَا ذكر ; إلا أن يقال : أنَّه مبني على الإطلاق الآخر المشار إليه بقولنا فيما سبق: «وقد يعبر أيضاً ... إلخ».
Al-Anbabi says: His statement: 'The lam in 'lillah' is either for worthiness... etc.' The difference between these three is that: (1) Lam al-istihqaq is 'that which occurs between a meaning and an essence'; for example: 'al-hamdu lillah', and 'Woe to the defrauders' [Quran 83:1] based on 'woe' being a name for punishment, not a valley in Hell. (2) Lam al-ikhtisas is 'that which occurs between two essences, and its object is not owned'; for example: 'the saddlecloth is for the beast'. Or 'between two essences and the companion of its object is not owned'; for example: 'for Zayd is a son' since the son is not owned, and 'you are for me and I am for you' when both the addressee and the speaker are free. The preponderant view is that what is meant by 'ikhtisas' here is connection and relation, not restriction. (3) Lam al-milk is 'that which occurs between two essences, and its object is owned'; for example: 'the wealth is for Zayd'. They also sometimes refer to the first and last as 'lam al-ikhtisas', and sometimes refer to the second as 'lam al-istihqaq'; this is the gist of what is in al-Ashmuni and the marginalia of the investigator al-Sabban. Based on this, making the lam here for milk is not apparent because 'al-hamd' is a meaning, not an essence. But some replied that making it for milk is one of two opinions, which is that it is not conditional that it be between two essences, even if that is contrary to the well-known, so investigate. Making it for ikhtisas is also not apparent for what was mentioned, unless it is said that it is based on the other usage referred to in our earlier statement: 'They also sometimes refer...'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by al-Anbabi on the earlier distinction of the three lam types. He clarifies each with definitions and examples. Lam al-istihqaq links a meaning (like praise or woe) to an essence (like Allah or the defrauders) and indicates that the meaning is deserved. Lam al-ikhtisas links two essences (like saddlecloth and beast) without ownership; the object of the lam (the beast) does not own the saddlecloth. A further example is 'for Zayd is a son' where the son is not owned property. Al-Anbabi notes that the preponderant view is that ikhtisas here means relation, not restriction. Lam al-milk also links two essences but the object is owned, like 'the wealth is for Zayd'. He then discusses terminological overlap: sometimes lam al-istihqaq and lam al-milk are both called lam al-ikhtisas, etc. This leads to a discussion about the applicability of lam al-milk to 'al-hamd lillah'. Since 'al-hamd' is a meaning rather than a concrete essence, some scholars object to lam al-milk here, while others accept it on a lenient interpretation. Al-Anbabi advises to investigate this matter carefully.

Key Terms
لام الاستحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah', the lam is best classified as which type?
A) Lam al-istihqaq
B) Lam al-ikhtisas
C) Lam al-milk
D) No lam

Key Takeaway
Lam al-istihqaq is defined as occurring between a meaning and an essence, indicating the meaning is deserved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which lam is used in 'Al-salatu li-llahi' (Prayer is for Allah). Is it lam al-istihqaq? Justify using the definition.
?
Find a sentence in classical Arabic or Quran where lam al-istihqaq occurs and explain why it fits.

Lam al-Ikhtisas: Connection Without Ownership
Al-Anbabi defines lam al-ikhtisas with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify lam al-ikhtisas in a sentence and explain its condition of non-ownership.

Core Idea
Lam al-ikhtisas links two essences without implying ownership.

This lesson covers lam al-ikhtisas, which connects two essences where the object of the lam is not owned. Al-Anbabi provides examples such as 'the saddlecloth is for the beast' and expands to cases like 'for Zayd is a son'. Understanding this lam is essential for parsing relationships in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Define lam al-ikhtisas
From the passage: 'occurring between two essences, and its object is not owned.' It denotes connection or relation, not possession.
The preponderant view is that ikhtisas here means relation, not restriction.
2
Analyze the example: 'al-jullu li-d-dabbati'
The saddlecloth (essence) is for the beast (essence), but the beast does not own the saddlecloth as property. It is a functional relationship.
3
Analyze the example: 'li-Zaydin ibnun'
'For Zayd is a son' – the son is not owned; it is a relational connection between two essences (Zayd and son).
Al-Anbabi extends this to 'anta li wa ana lak' when both are free.
4
Distinguish from lam al-milk
Both involve two essences, but lam al-milk requires the object to be owned, whereas lam al-ikhtisas does not.
This is the critical distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Lam al-Milk: Ownership Between Essences
Al-Anbabi defines lam al-milk with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify lam al-milk in a sentence and verify the ownership condition.

Core Idea
Lam al-milk signals possession between two essences.

Lam al-milk is the third type, indicating ownership. Al-Anbabi defines it as occurring between two essences where the object is owned. This lam is common in legal contexts and everyday speech. Mastering it ensures accurate interpretation of possession in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Define lam al-milk
From the passage: 'occurring between two essences, and its object is owned.' This lam asserts that the second term (the object) is owned by the first.
Example: 'al-malu li-Zaydin' (the wealth is for Zayd).
2
Analyze the example: 'al-malu li-Zaydin'
Wealth (essence) is owned by Zayd (essence). This indicates a legal ownership relationship.
3
Compare with lam al-ikhtisas
Both involve two essences, but lam al-milk requires ownership, while lam al-ikhtisas does not. The same phrase can differ in meaning based on context.
For instance, 'al-kitabu li-muhammadin' could be milk (ownership) or ikhtisas (temporary use) depending on intent.
4
Note the terminological overlap
Al-Anbabi notes that sometimes lam al-milk is also called lam al-ikhtisas in a broader sense. However, the narrow distinction is based on ownership.
This overlap is important for reading advanced texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Al-Anbabi says: His statement: 'The lam in 'lillah' is either for worthiness... etc.' The difference between these three is that: (1) Lam al-istihqaq is 'that which occurs between a meaning and an essence'; for example: 'al-hamdu lillah', and 'Woe to the defrauders' [Quran 83:1] based on 'woe' being a name for punishment, not a valley in Hell. Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by al-Anbabi on the earlier distinction of the three lam types. He clarifies each with definitions and examples. Lam al-istihqaq links a meaning (like praise or woe) to an essence (like Allah or the defrauders) and indicates that the meaning is deserved. Lam al-ikhtisas links two essences (like saddlecloth and beast) without ownership; the object of the lam (the beast) does not own the saddlecloth. A further example is 'for Zayd is a son' where the son is not owned property. Al-Anbabi notes that the preponderant view is that ikhtisas here means relation, not restriction. Lam al-milk also links two essences but the object is owned, like 'the wealth is for Zayd'. He then discusses terminological overlap: sometimes lam al-istihqaq and lam al-milk are both called lam al-ikhtisas, etc. This leads to a discussion about the applicability of lam al-milk to 'al-hamd lillah'. Since 'al-hamd' is a meaning rather than a concrete essence, some scholars object to lam al-milk here, while others accept it on a lenient interpretation. Al-Anbabi advises to investigate this matter carefully.

Key Terms
لام الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Lam al-istihqaq
B) Lam al-ikhtisas
C) Lam al-milk
D) None

Key Takeaway
Lam al-milk denotes ownership between two essences.

The Debate: Which Lam in 'Al-hamd lillah'?
Al-Anbabi discusses the classification of lam in 'al-hamd lillah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the arguments for different classifications of lam in 'al-hamd lillah'.

Core Idea
Classifying the lam in 'al-hamd lillah' hinges on whether 'al-hamd' is a meaning or an essence.

Al-Anbabi discusses a grammatical debate: some scholars consider the lam in 'al-hamd lillah' as lam al-milk or lam al-ikhtisas, but the preferred view is lam al-istihqaq. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position, sharpening your analytical skills.

Guided Path
1
State the problem
'Al-hamd' is a meaning (praise), not a concrete essence. Lam al-milk requires two essences with ownership. So why would some classify it as milk?
2
Examine the argument for lam al-milk
Some scholars allow lam al-milk even when one side is a meaning, relaxing the condition of two essences. Al-Anbaji notes this is a minority opinion.
They argue that the lam indicates that praise is 'owned' by Allah metaphorically.
3
Examine the argument for lam al-ikhtisas
Others consider it lam al-ikhtisas based on a broader usage where the term includes lam al-istihqaq. They see it as connecting Allah to praise without ownership.
Al-Anbaji points out that this is based on a terminological overlap.
4
Evaluate the preferred view: lam al-istihqaq
Al-Anbaji favors lam al-istihqaq because the definition fits perfectly: a meaning (praise) linked to an essence (Allah), indicating desert. It avoids the need for metaphorical or broad interpretations.
This is the most straightforward classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأنبابي قوله : (فَـ «اللَّامُ فِي اللهِ »: إِمَّا لِلِاسْتِحْقَاقِ ... إلخ) الفرقُ بين هذه الثلاثة : أنَّ : (۱) - لامَ الاستحقاق هي: «الواقعةُ بين معنى وذات»؛ نحو: «الحمد لله، و: ﴿وَيْلٌ لِلْمُطَفِّفِينَ﴾ (المطففين: ١)؛ بناءً على أنَّ «الوَيْلَ» اسمٌ للعذاب، لا على أنَّه اسم وادٍ في جهنَّم. (۲) - ولامَ الاختصاص هي : «الواقعةُ بين ذاتين، ومدخولها لا يملك»؛ نحو: «الجُلُّ لِلدَّابَّةِ». أو : «بين ذاتين ومصاحب مدخولها لا يملك»؛ نحو: «لِزَيْدٍ ابْنُ»؛ إذ الابنُ لا يملك، و: «أَنْتَ لِي وَأَنَا لَكَ» إذا كان كلٌّ مِنَ المخاطب والمتكلّم حرا . والرَّاجِحُ : أنَّ المراد بـ «الاختصاص» هنا : التَّعلُّق والارتباط، لا القصر. (۳) - ولام الملك هي : «الواقعةُ بین ذاتين، ومدخولها يملك»؛ نحو: «المَالُ لِزَيْدِ». وقد يعبر أيضاً عن الأول والأخير بـ: «لام الاختصاص، كما أنَّـه قـد يـعـبـر عـن الـثـانـي بـ «لام الاستحقاق» ؛ هذا حاصل ما في الأشموني وحاشية المحقق الصَّبَّان عليه وعلى هذا، فجعل اللَّام هنا للملك لا يظهر ; لأنَّ الحمد معنى لا ذات، وأجاب بعضهم : بأن جعلها هنا للملك أحد قولين، وهو أنَّه لا يشترط أن تكون بين ذاتين، وإن كان خلاف المشهور، فحرّر . وجعلها للاختصاص لا يظهر أيضاً ; لِمَا ذكر ; إلا أن يقال : أنَّه مبني على الإطلاق الآخر المشار إليه بقولنا فيما سبق: «وقد يعبر أيضاً ... إلخ».
Al-Anbabi says: His statement: 'The lam in 'lillah' is either for worthiness... etc.' The difference between these three is that: (1) Lam al-istihqaq is 'that which occurs between a meaning and an essence'; for example: 'al-hamdu lillah', and 'Woe to the defrauders' [Quran 83:1] based on 'woe' being a name for punishment, not a valley in Hell. (2) Lam al-ikhtisas is 'that which occurs between two essences, and its object is not owned'; for example: 'the saddlecloth is for the beast'. Or 'between two essences and the companion of its object is not owned'; for example: 'for Zayd is a son' since the son is not owned, and 'you are for me and I am for you' when both the addressee and the speaker are free. The preponderant view is that what is meant by 'ikhtisas' here is connection and relation, not restriction. (3) Lam al-milk is 'that which occurs between two essences, and its object is owned'; for example: 'the wealth is for Zayd'. They also sometimes refer to the first and last as 'lam al-ikhtisas', and sometimes refer to the second as 'lam al-istihqaq'; this is the gist of what is in al-Ashmuni and the marginalia of the investigator al-Sabban. Based on this, making the lam here for milk is not apparent because 'al-hamd' is a meaning, not an essence. But some replied that making it for milk is one of two opinions, which is that it is not conditional that it be between two essences, even if that is contrary to the well-known, so investigate. Making it for ikhtisas is also not apparent for what was mentioned, unless it is said that it is based on the other usage referred to in our earlier statement: 'They also sometimes refer...'

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by al-Anbabi on the earlier distinction of the three lam types. He clarifies each with definitions and examples. Lam al-istihqaq links a meaning (like praise or woe) to an essence (like Allah or the defrauders) and indicates that the meaning is deserved. Lam al-ikhtisas links two essences (like saddlecloth and beast) without ownership; the object of the lam (the beast) does not own the saddlecloth. A further example is 'for Zayd is a son' where the son is not owned property. Al-Anbabi notes that the preponderant view is that ikhtisas here means relation, not restriction. He then discusses terminological overlap: sometimes lam al-istihqaq and lam al-milk are both called lam al-ikhtisas, etc. This leads to a discussion about the applicability of lam al-milk to 'al-hamd lillah'. Since 'al-hamd' is a meaning rather than a concrete essence, some scholars object to lam al-milk here, while others accept it on a lenient interpretation. Al-Anbabi advises to investigate this matter carefully.

The Interpretation of 'Akhraja' in the Poet's Line
Author comments on the matn's first line, interpreting 'akhraja' as 'ijad' (creation) rather than 'izhar' (manifestation).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of 'ikhraj' (izhar vs. ijad) and explain why the commentator prefers creation over manifestation.

Core Idea
The word 'akhraja' does not simply mean 'revealed' – it means 'created' in the logical poem.

In the opening line of the matn, the poet uses the word 'akhraja' (brought forth). A commentator, al-Malawi, interpreted it as 'izhar' (manifestation), but the annotator argues that a better interpretation is 'ijad' (creation). This brief exchange illustrates how a single term can carry different logical nuances, and how commentators refine each other's understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under discussion
Look for the word 'akhraja' (أَخْرَجَا) in the poet's line. It is the first line of the matn.
2
Understand al-Malawi's interpretation
Al-Malawi interpreted 'ikhraj' as 'izhar' (making apparent). This is one possible meaning.
3
Understand the commentator's preferred interpretation
The commentator says it is better to interpret 'ikhraj' as 'ijad' (creation). Note the comparative: 'al-ahsan' means 'better'.
4
Examine the marginal note's evaluation
The marginal note claims that al-Malawi's interpretation is better for several reasons, though those reasons are not given here. This shows layered commentary.
The footnote marker (7) indicates a marginal note elsewhere.
5
Reflect on the theological/logical implications
Consider why 'ijad' might be more precise: logic deals with creating concepts in the mind, not just manifesting them.

Commentary
This passage is a brief commentary on the first line of the matn (the poem), specifically on the word 'akhraja' (brought forth). The commentator notes that the word is written with an 'alif' at the end, indicating the absolute form (alif al-itlaq). He then mentions that al-Shaykh al-Malawi interpreted 'al-ikhraj' as 'al-izhar' (manifestation or making apparent), but the commentator prefers to interpret it as 'al-ijad' (creation or bringing into existence). The text then quotes the marginal commentator (possibly al-Anbabi) who says that what al-Malawi did is better for several reasons, though those reasons are not fully given in the passage. This illustrates a typical logical commentary: the commentator clarifies the meaning of a key term in the matn, offering his preferred interpretation, and then a super-commentator critiques or supports that preference.

Refining Interpretation: Ijād vs. Izhār
Commentator critiques al-Mallawi's interpretation and argues for 'ijād' over 'izhār'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the commentator's argument for preferring 'ijād' over 'izhār' and assess al-Anbabi's counterarguments.

Core Idea
The choice between 'bringing into existence' and 'making manifest' determines whether divine action creates or merely reveals.

In this critical passage, the commentator examines al-Mallawi's interpretation of the poet's phrase 'alladhī qad akhraja'. He argues that 'akhraja' should be understood as 'bringing into existence' (ijād) rather than 'making manifest' (izhār), based on both emphatic strength and theological consistency. However, al-Anbabi raises three points in defense of 'izhār', and the commentator responds. This lesson guides you through the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Locate al-Mallawi's interpretation ('izhār') and the commentator's preferred interpretation ('ijād') in the Arabic passage.
They are explicitly contrasted in the first lines of the commentary.
2
Extract the commentator's reasons
The commentator gives two reasons: 'ijād' is more emphatic (ablagh), and 'izhār' implies pre-existence of what is brought forth, which is not appropriate here.
Look for the phrases 'ablagh' and 'shān al-iẓhār anna yakūn li-mawjūdin qablu'.
3
Analyze al-Anbabi's counterarguments
Al-Anbabi lists three points: (a) the subsequent line about 'appearing' suits manifestation, (b) praise for bringing into existence is implied by praise for manifestation, (c) the results exist in thought, so only manifestation is needed.
These are introduced with 'minhā' (among them).
4
Evaluate the resolution
The commentator counters by noting the real target is the Mu'tazila who deny creation of essences, and the relative clause creates rhetorical ambiguity then clarity.
The passage ends with the commentator's justification of the relative clause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا) بِأَلِفِ الإِطْلَاقِ. وَقَدْ فَسَّرَ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ الإِخْرَاجَ بـ : الإِظْهَارِ ، وَالأَحْسَنُ أَنْ يُفَسَّرَ بِ: «الإِيْجَادِ»؛ لأَنَّهُ أَبْلَغُ مِنَ الإِظْهَارِ ، وَلِأَنَّ شَأنَ الإِظْهَارِ أَنَّهُ يَكُونُ لِمَوْجُودٍ قَبْلُ، وَمَا هُنَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ. وَ«قَدْ» : لِلتَّحْقِيقِ ، وَمِنَ المَعْلُومِ أَنَّ المَوْصُولَ مَعَ صِلَتِهِ فِي قُوَّةِ المُشْتَقِّ، فَقَوْلُهُ: «الَّذِي قَدْ أَخْرَجَا» فِي قوَّةِ : المُخْرِجِ ، وَلَمْ يُعَبِّرْ بِهِ مَعَ وُرُودِ إِطْلَاقِهِ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى خِلَافًا لِمَنْ زَعَمَ عَدَمَ وُرُودِهِ ، قَالَ تَعَالَى : ﴿وَاللَّهُ تُخْرِجُ مَا كُنتُمْ تَكْتُمُونَ﴾ [البقرة : ۷۲] ؛ لَعَلَّهُ : لِعَدَم شُهْرَتِهِ ، وَعَدَمِ ذِكْرِهِ فِي الأَسْمَاءِ الحُسْنَى المَعْرُوفَةِ .
الأنبابي: منها : مناسبة قوله : (حَتَّى بَدَتْ أي ظهرت)؛ لأنَّ بدو شموس المعرفة الشاملة للنتائج وغيرها يترتب على إظهارها، لا على محض الإيجاد مع خَفائها . ومنها : أنَّ الحمد على الإيجاد يُفهم بالأولى مِنَ الحمد على الإظهار. ومنها : أن قوله : (وَلِأَنَّ شَأنَ الإِظْهَارِ أَنَّهُ يَكُونُ لِمَوْجُودٍ قَبْلُ، وَمَا هُنَا لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ) لا يسلَّم؛ لأنَّ النتائج مثبتةٌ في أجزاء القياس، فهي موجودة أولاً بوجود الفكر، والذي يـحـصـل بـعـد ذلـك إنَّما هو الإظهار .
قوله : (لِأَنَّهُ أَبْلَغُ ... إلخ) أي : وللرَّدِّ على من يقول: «الماهيات ليست بجعل جاعل، وإنما الله تعالى أظهرها فقط». 
قوله : (لِعَدَم شُهْرَتِهِ ... إلخ) أو يقال : إنَّ في التعبير بالموصول المستقل وصلته إبهاماً صريحاً ثمَّ تفصيلاً، وهو أوقع في النَّفس .
His statement: (The One who has brought forth) with the alif of absoluteness. And the teacher al-Mallawi interpreted 'bringing forth' as 'making manifest', but it is better to interpret it as 'bringing into existence'; because it is more emphatic than 'making manifest', and because the quality of making manifest is that it is for something that already exists, and what is here is not like that. And 'qad' is for verification. And it is known that the relative pronoun with its clause is in the strength of a derived noun, so his statement 'the One who has brought forth' is in the strength of 'the Bringer forth'. And he did not use that expression despite its application to Him, may He be exalted, contrary to those who claim it is not applied, for Allah said: 'And Allah brings forth what you were concealing' (2:72). Perhaps because of its lack of fame and its absence from the well-known beautiful names.
Al-Anbabi: Some points: the suitability of his statement (until it appeared, i.e., became manifest) because the appearing of the suns of comprehensive knowledge including results and others depends on making them manifest, not on mere bringing into existence while they are hidden. And another: that praise for bringing into existence is understood by way of priority from praise for making manifest. And another: that his statement, 'the quality of making manifest is that it is for something that already exists, and what is here is not like that,' is not granted, because the results are established in the parts of the syllogism, so they exist initially through the existence of thought, and what occurs afterwards is only making manifest.
His statement: (because it is more emphatic, etc.) means: and in response to those who say: 'The essences are not made by a maker, but rather Allah only made them manifest.'
His statement: (perhaps because of its lack of fame) or it is said: that in using the independent relative pronoun and its clause there is explicit ambiguity then detail, and it is more impactful on the soul.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary where the super-commentator (al-Anbabi) refines and evaluates the interpretation of a key word in the opening line of the poem. The poet says 'Allah who has brought forth (akhraja) the fruits of thought'. The earlier commentator al-Mallawi interpreted 'akhraja' as 'making manifest' (izhār). However, the main commentator argues that the better interpretation is 'bringing into existence' (ijād) for two reasons: (1) it is more emphatic (ablagh), implying a more comprehensive action; (2) making manifest presupposes pre-existence, but the divine act here is creative, not merely revealing. He then discusses the word 'qad' which emphasizes verification. He also notes that the relative clause 'alladhī qad akhraja' functions like a derived noun 'mukhrij' (the bringer forth), but the poet chose the relative clause instead of the noun, perhaps because the noun 'mukhrij' is not among the well-known beautiful names of Allah, though the Quran does use the verb 'yukhrij'.

Al-Anbabi then lists three points that some might raise in defense of al-Mallawi's interpretation: (a) the poet's subsequent line 'until the suns of knowledge appeared' fits better with 'making manifest' because appearance follows manifestation, not mere existence; (b) praise for bringing into existence is implied by praise for making manifest (priority by implication); (c) the argument that making manifest requires prior existence is not conceded because the 'results' are inherent in the parts of the syllogism and exist in thought, so making manifest is exactly what happens. The commentator then counters these points: the real intended opponent is the Mu'tazila rationalist who denies that essences are created by God, claiming God only reveals them. By choosing 'bringing into existence', the poet refutes them directly. Additionally, the use of the relative clause creates an effect of initial ambiguity then clarity, which is more rhetorically powerful.

Thus, the passage exemplifies a nuanced debate over word choice in theological context, showing how logic and theology intersect in commentary literature.

Key Terms
الإخراج
الإظهار
الإيجاد
الموصول مع صلته

Checkpoint
Why does al-Anbabi raise the point about the subsequent line 'until it appeared'?
A) To show that the poet's wording supports 'izhār' over 'ijād'.
B) To undermine the commentator's argument for 'ijād'.
C) To support the poet's use of 'ijād'.
D) To introduce a new interpretation of 'akhraja'.

Key Takeaway
The interpretive choice between 'bringing into existence' and 'making manifest' hinges on theological commitments about divine action and creation, as illustrated by the commentator's critique of al-Mallawi.

Objection and Answer on the Cause of Praise
Author addresses two objections about the causal link between 'bringing out' and 'praise'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between eternal praise and temporal initiation of praise based on the logical rule of derived terms.

Core Idea
Not all praise is caused; only the creature's act of praising is causally linked to a specific blessing.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection to the rule that a judgment attached to a derived term implies causality. The objection concerns the phrase 'alhamdu lillah alladhi akhraja'—does it mean that the act of bringing out is the cause of all praise? The answer reveals a crucial distinction: only the temporal act of praising (insha' al-thana') is caused, not the eternal attribute of praise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical rule
Focus on the opening statement: 'It is a rule that the attachment of a judgment to a derived term indicates the causality of that from which it is derived.' This is a core principle in usul al-fiqh.
The derived term here is 'alladhi akhraja' from 'ikhraj'.
2
Apply the rule to the phrase
The phrase 'alhamdu lillah alladhi akhraja' attaches the judgment 'alhamdu' (praise) to the derived term 'alladhi akhraja' (who brought out). According to the rule, this suggests that the act of bringing out (al-ikhraj) is the cause of the praise.
3
Recognize the objection
The objector argues that 'praise' includes eternal praise (al-hamd al-qadim), which is uncaused. Since the rule would imply that even eternal praise is caused by the bringing out, a contradiction arises.
The objection assumes that 'al-hamd' in the phrase refers to all praise, including God's self-praise.
4
Understand the answer's refinement
The answer specifies that what is caused is only the initiation of praise (insha' al-thana'), not the eternal praise. This refined understanding resolves the contradiction: the creature's temporal act of praising is causally linked to the specific blessing, while the eternal praise remains uncaused.
The same answer addresses a second objection: that temporal beings praise God for His attributes, not for specific acts. The response reaffirms that the specific act of praising is caused by the specific blessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قِيْلَ : مِنَ القَوَاعِدِ أَنَّ تَعْلِيقَ الحُكْمِ بِالمُشْتَقِّ أَوْ مَا فِي قُوَّتِهِ يُوْذِنُ بِعِلِّيَّةِ مَا مِنْهُ الِاشْتِقَاقُ، فَتَقْتَضِي العِبَارَةُ عِليَّةَ الإِخْرَاجِ لِلحَمْدِ ، مَعَ أَنَّ المُتَبَادَرَ أَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ «الحَمْدِ»: مَا يَشْمَلُ الحَمْدَ القَدِيمَ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلٍ. أَجِيْبَ : بِأَنَّ المُعَلَّلَ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ إِنْشَاءُ الثَّناءِ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ. وَبِهَذَا يُجَابُ أَيْضاً عَمَّا يُقَالُ: يَرِدُ عَلَى العِليَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ أَنَّ حَمْدَ الحَوَادِثِ لَهُ تَعَالَى لَيْسَ لِخُصُوص ذَلِكَ، بَلْ لِكَوْنِهِ الإِلَهَ الحَقَّ المُنْعِمَ بِجَمِيعِ النِّعَمِ المُتَّصِفِ بِالصِّفَاتِ الجَمِيلَةِ
For it is said: It is a rule that the attachment of a judgment to a derived term or what is in its power indicates the causality of that from which it is derived. So the expression necessitates the causality of the bringing out for the praise, while the apparent meaning is that what is intended by 'praise' includes eternal praise, and that is not caused. The answer is: What is actually caused is only the initiation of praise, as preceded. And with this an answer is also given to what is said: There is an objection against the mentioned causality that the praise of temporal beings for Him, exalted, is not because of that specific thing, but because of His being the true God, the bestower of all blessings, the one described with beautiful attributes.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the deductive rule that linking a judgment to a derived term (like 'who brought out') indicates a cause. The objector argues that the phrase 'alhamdu lillah alladhi akhraja' implies the act of bringing out is the cause of the praise, but the praise includes eternal praise (God's own praise of Himself) which has no cause. The answer refines the understanding: what is actually caused is not the eternal praise itself, but the temporal act of praising (insha' al-thana') by the creature, specifically the author of the poem. This act is indeed caused by the bringing out of the fruits of thought. The same answer applies to a second objection: that the praise of temporal beings is not specifically for that act but for God's essential attributes. The response maintains that the praised act (the act of praising) is still causally linked to the specific blessing, while the eternal praise remains uncaused.

Four Stages of the Intellect in Islamic Philosophy
Philosophers classify the intellect into four stages: material, active, acquired, and actual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four stages of the intellect (hayulani, fa''al, mustafad, bi-al-fi'l) and explain how the soul progresses from potential to actual knowledge.

Core Idea
The intellect develops through four stages: raw potential, sensory preparation, active acquisition, and stored mastery.

The philosophers (hukama') held that the intellect and soul are one, and they described the soul's journey from being empty of knowledge to having knowledge readily available. This lesson takes you through their four-stage classification, which is fundamental to understanding the Islamic epistemological tradition of how human beings come to know.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Starting Point
Note that the soul begins empty of all sciences but is prepared (musta'iddah) to receive them. This potential state is the foundation.
The analogy to matter (hayula) emphasizes pure receptivity.
2
Trace the First Stage: Material Intellect
When the soul is at this potential stage, it is called 'material intellect' (al-'aql al-hayulani). It has no actual knowledge but has the capacity.
3
Move to the Second Stage: Active Intellect
When the soul uses its senses (external and internal) to obtain primary sciences (badihiyyat) and becomes ready for theoretical knowledge, it is called 'active intellect' (al-'aql al-fa''al).
This stage involves active engagement with the world.
4
Understand the Third Stage: Acquired Intellect
After arranging primary sciences and directly perceiving theoretical ones, the soul is called 'acquired intellect' (al-'aql al-mustafad) because it benefits from the active intellect.
This is the stage of actual understanding.
5
Recognize the Fourth Stage: Actual Intellect
When knowledge becomes stored and can be recalled at will without new effort, it is called 'actual intellect' (al-'aql bi-al-fi'l). Knowledge is now a habit (malakah).
This is the goal of learning: ready knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا مبنيٌّ على تغاير العقل والنَّفس، وعليه فالنَّفْسُ : مَعْنِّى لَطِيفٌ رَبَّانِيٌّ بِهِ حَيَاةُ الإِنْسَانِ». وذهب الحكماء إلى اتحادهما، وقسموا النفس أربعة أقسام فقالوا: (۱) ـ إنَّها في مبدأ الفطرة خالية عن العلوم كلها، لكنَّها مستعدة لها، وإلا لامتنع اتصافها بها، وحينئذ تسمى : عقلاً هَيُوليا»؛ تشبيهاً لها بالهَيُولى الخالية في نفسها عن جميع الصُّور القابلة لها . (۲) - ثمَّ إذا استعملت آلاتها - أعني : الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة ، وحصل لها علوم أوَّليَّة، واستعدت لاكتساب النَّظريَّات سميت بـ: «العقل الفعّال». (۳) - ثمَّ إذا رتَّبت العلوم الأوَّليَّة، وأدركتِ النَّظريَّات مشاهدة لها سمّيت بـ: «العقل المستفاد»؛ لاستفادته مِنَ العقل الفعّال . ( ٤ ) - وإذا صارت مخزونةً عندها، وحصلت لها مَلَكةُ الاستحضار متى شاءت من غير تجشم جديد سميت : «عقلاً بالفعل».
And this is based on the distinction between intellect and soul, and according to that the soul is a subtle, lordly meaning by which is the life of the human. And the philosophers held that they are one, and divided the soul into four divisions and said: (1) At the beginning of innate nature, it is empty of all sciences, but it is prepared for them, otherwise it would be impossible for it to be characterized by them, and then it is called 'material intellect' (al-'aql al-hayulani), by analogy to matter which is empty in itself of all forms that are receptive to it. (2) Then, when it uses its instruments—namely the external and internal senses—and obtains primary sciences, and is prepared to acquire theoretical ones, it is called 'active intellect' (al-'aql al-fa''al). (3) Then, when it arranges the primary sciences and perceives the theoretical ones as witnessed, it is called 'acquired intellect' (al-'aql al-mustafad), because it benefits from the active intellect. (4) And when they become stored in it, and it acquires the property of recalling them whenever it wishes without new effort, it is called 'actual intellect' (al-'aql bi-al-fi'l).
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of the intellect into four stages as held by the philosophers (hukama'). It begins from the premise that the soul is a subtle, lordly principle of life. The philosophers, contrary to the distinction made earlier, posit the unity of intellect and soul. They then divide the soul into four stages: (1) material intellect ('aql hayulani) — the potential state at birth, empty of knowledge but ready to receive it; (2) active intellect ('aql fa''al) — when the soul uses the senses to acquire primary sciences and prepares for theoretical ones; (3) acquired intellect ('aql mustafad) — when the soul actively contemplates and perceives theoretical knowledge directly; and (4) actual intellect ('aql bi-al-fi'l) — when knowledge becomes stored and easily recalled. The analogy to matter (hayula) illustrates the receptive potential of the first stage.

Key Terms
العقل الهيولاني
العقل الفعال
العقل المستفاد
العقل بالفعل
النفس

Key Takeaway
The philosophers view the intellect as progressing through four stages: material (potential), active (sensory preparation), acquired (direct knowledge), and actual (stored mastery).

Distinguishing Efficient and Final Causes in Divine Acts
Author prefers one interpretation to avoid the objection that God's acts are uncaused, then defines final cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an efficient cause (ʿilla bāʿitha) and a final cause (ʿilla ghāʾiyya) in the context of divine actions, and define related terms (ghāya, gharaḍ, fāʾida) with precision.

Core Idea
God’s acts have wisdom (final cause) but are not compelled (efficient cause).

In this passage, the commentator responds to an objection that divine acts cannot be caused. He clarifies that the objection only applies to efficient causes, not final causes. You will learn to make this critical distinction and to precisely define key terms like 'end', 'purpose', and 'benefit' as they relate to final causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of the line
The text mentions two interpretations of 'hatta' (removal). The first is preferred because the second invites the objection that God's acts are uncaused.
The objection is that the actions of Allah are not subject to causation.
2
Understand the distinction between efficient and final cause
The commentator responds to the objection by distinguishing between an efficient cause (ʿilla bāʿitha), which compels the agent, and a final cause (ʿilla ghāʾiyya), which is the wisdom or benefit of the action. The objection applies only to efficient causes.
An efficient cause is denied of God because He is not compelled; a final cause affirms divine wisdom.
3
Examine the definitions of ghāya, gharaḍ, and fā'ida
The passage defines three terms based on the aspect (ḥaythiyya): ghāya is the benefit considered as the result of the action; gharaḍ is that benefit as sought by the agent; fā'ida is the benefit as the fruit of the action. The final cause (ʿilla ghāʾiyya) is the benefit as a motivator.
These distinctions refine how we talk about benefit in relation to action.
4
Apply the distinction to a real case
Consider the divine act of giving knowledge. It is not caused by an efficient cause (God is not compelled), but it has a final cause: the benefit of removing ignorance. This demonstrates why the first interpretation is sound.
The removal of veils (kashf) is the production of knowledge, which is a benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالأَوَّلُ - أَعْنِي : جَعْلَهُ مِنْ عَطْفِ السَّبَبِ عَلَى المُسَبَّبِ - أَوْلَى ؛ لِمَا يَرِدُ عَلَى الثَّانِي مِنْ أَنَّ أَفْعَالَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى لَا تُعَلَّلُ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ لَا تَخْلُو عَنْ حِكْمَةٍ. قوله : (مِنْ أَنَّ أَفْعَالَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى لَا تُعَلَّلُ) فيه : أنَّ أفعال الله تعالى لا تُعلَّل بعِلَّةٍ باعثة، وما هنا علةٌ غائيَّةٌ. إلا أن يقال : ربَّما يتوهَّم أنَّ المراد هنا : العِلَّة الباعثة، أفاده شيخنا المؤلّف. وهذا يقتضي أنَّ أفعال الله تعالى تعلل بالعِلل الغائيَّة، وكلامُهُ الآتي في تفسير العلة الغائيَّة يقتضي المنع، وعبارته عند قول المصنف: «فَوَائِدا» : لغةً: ما استفدته من علم أو مال، أو نحوهما»، واصطلاحاً: والفوائد جمع : «فَائِدَةٍ»، وهي المصلحة المترتبة على الفعل من حيث إنها ثمرته ونتيجته. - و خرج بالحيثية المذكورة : الغاية، فإنَّها تلك المصلحة من حيث إنَّها في طرف الفعل. والغرض، فإنَّه المصلحة المذكورة من حيث إنَّها مطلوبة للفاعل مِنَ الفِعل. والعِلَّة الغائيّة، فإنّها تلك المصلحة من حيث إنّها باعثةٌ للفاعل على الفعل.
And the first – I mean: making it from the conjunction of the cause upon the effect – is more appropriate, due to what occurs upon the second [interpretation] from [the objection] that the actions of Allah (exalted) are not caused, even though they are not devoid of wisdom. His statement: (from that the actions of Allah are not caused) in it: that the actions of Allah are not caused by an efficient cause, and what is here is a final cause. However, it could be said: perhaps it might be imagined that what is intended here is the efficient cause, as our teacher, the author, informed us. And this implies that the actions of Allah are caused by final causes, and his upcoming speech in the interpretation of final cause implies prohibition. And his statement at the author's saying: 'Benefits': linguistically: what you have benefited from knowledge or wealth or the like, and technically: benefits is plural of 'benefit', which is the interest resulting from the action in terms of it being its fruit and result. – And by the mentioned respect is excluded: the end, for it is that interest in terms of it being on the side of the action. And the purpose, for it is the mentioned interest in terms of it being sought by the agent from the action. And the final cause, for it is that interest in terms of it being a motivator for the agent to act.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange. The author initially argues that the first interpretation of the line (connecting the cause to the effect) is preferable because the second interpretation invites the objection that God's actions are not subject to causation. The commentator then responds by distinguishing between two types of causes: an efficient cause (ʿilla bāʿitha) which compels the agent, and a final cause (ʿilla ghāʾiyya) which is the purpose or wisdom behind the action. The objection applies only to efficient causes, while the 'removal of veils' here is a final cause – the removal of ignorance is itself the production of knowledge. The text then defines key terms: al-ghāya (the end) is the benefit considered as the outcome of the action; al-gharaḍ (the purpose) is that benefit as sought by the agent; and al-ʿilla al-ghāʾiyya (the final cause) is that benefit as it motivates the agent. The commentator notes that later statements by the author seem to deny even final causes for God, leading to a subtle tension. The intention is to clarify that the objection does not undermine the preferred interpretation, because what is meant here is not an efficient cause but a final cause, which is consistent with divine wisdom.

Key Takeaway

Grammatical Parsing of Badal and Preposition Substitution
Author parses the line grammatically and explains the metaphor of intellect as a sky for knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the two types of badal (substitute) and the Kufan doctrine of preposition interchange in Arabic grammatical analysis.

Core Idea
Arabic grammar allows precise substitution: one phrase can replace another as a grammatical substitute (badal) and prepositions can interchange.

In this passage, the commentator parses the phrase 'min sama' al-'aql' as a substitute (badal) for the earlier prepositional phrase. Two types of badal are considered: badal ishtimal (substitute of inclusion) and badal ba'd min kull (part for whole). Additionally, the particle 'min' is interpreted as meaning 'an' (from) according to the Kufan school, which permits one preposition to take the place of another. Understanding these grammatical nuances is crucial for correctly interpreting classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base phrase being substituted
Find the original prepositional phrase that 'min sama' al-'aql' replaces. It is the earlier phrase in the text (likely 'anhum'). Notice how the substitute must agree grammatically with the substituted phrase.
The substitute and the substituted are in the same case and function.
2
Distinguish the two types of badal
Badal ishtimal indicates the substitute is inclusive of the first (e.g., 'the man, his knowledge'), while badal ba'd min kull indicates the substitute is a part of the whole (e.g., 'the ship, its sail'). Here the commentator prefers badal ishtimal.
The relationship between 'aql (intellect) and arbab al-hija (people of intellect) is one of inclusion: intellect is an attribute of its people.
3
Analyze the preposition substitution
The Kufan school allows 'min' to mean 'an' (from). Check if this interpretation makes sense contextually: 'from the intellect' vs 'from the people of intellect'. The substitution changes the meaning slightly.
This is not universally accepted; the Basran school generally forbids preposition interchange.
4
Apply the parsing to the full phrase
Conclude that 'min sama' al-'aql' functions as a badal ishtimal of the previous jar wa majrur, and 'min' carries the meaning of 'an' as per Kufan grammar.
This parsing is essential for understanding the syntax of the original line.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (عَنْهُمْ) أَيْ: عَنْ أَرْبَابِ الحِجَا . وَقَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ) سَمَاءِ العَقْلِ بَدَلٌ مِنَ الجَارِّ وَالمَجْرُورِ قَبْلَهُ ؛ بَدَلُ اشْتِمَالٍ، أَوْ بَدَلُ بَعْضٍ مِنْ كُلِّ، وَالأَوَّلُ أَقْرَبُ . وَ(مِنْ)) وَ مِنْ بِمَعْنَى : «عَنْ» عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الكُوفِيِّينَ ؛ مِنْ تَجْوِيزِ نِيَابَةِ بَعْضِ الحُرُوفِ عَنْ بَعْضٍ. وَأَشَارَ المُصَنِّفُ فِي شَرْحِهِ إِلَى أَنَّ إِضَافَةَ سَمَاءِ إِلَى العَقْلِ مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ إِلَى المُشَبِّهِ. وَالأَصْلُ: «مِنَ العَقْلِ الَّذِي هُوَ كَالسَّمَاءِ؛ بِجَامِعِ : أَنَّ كُلًّا مَحَلٌّ لِطُلُوعِ الشَّمُوسِ. قوله : (مَجَازاً بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ هذا لا يتعيَّن بل يصح أن يكون مرسلاً لعلاقة التقييد ثم الإطلاق؛ بأن تطلق الإزاحة الحسيَّة عن التّقييد بالحسيَّة، وتستعمل فى المعنوية. ولك أن تقول : ليس هنا إلا مجاز واحدٌ؛ بأن يُنقل الحظ عن الإزاحة الحسيَّة مِن عُلْوِ إِلَى سُفْلِ إلى مطلق إزاحة حسيَّة أو لا، من علو إلى سفل أو لا، ويستعمل في المعنوية؛ لكونها فرداً من الأفراد.
His statement: ('anhum) meaning: from the people of intellect. And his statement: (min) sama' al-'aql is a substitute for the prepositional phrase before it; a substitute of inclusion, or a substitute of part from whole, and the first is closer. And (min) and min means 'from' according to the Kufan school of allowing one particle to take the place of another. And the author indicated in his commentary that the attribution of 'sky' to 'intellect' is an attribution of the thing compared to the thing compared to. The original is: 'from the intellect which is like the sky' due to the commonality that each is a place for the rising of suns. He said: (metaphorically by metaphor) this is not determined, but it is valid for it to be a transferred metaphor through the relation of restriction then generalization; in that the physical removal is freed from the restriction of being physical and is used in the spiritual sense. And you can say: there is only one metaphor here; by transferring the term for physical removal from height to depth to absolute physical removal or not, and it is used in the spiritual sense because it is an individual of the individuals.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and rhetorical analysis of the line from the text. The commentator first explains that 'anhum' refers to 'the people of intellect' (arbab al-hija). Then he parses 'min sama' al-'aql' as a substitute (badal) for the earlier prepositional phrase, with two possibilities: badal ishtimal (substitute of inclusion) or badal ba'd min kull (part for whole), preferring the former. He also notes that 'min' here can mean 'an' (from) according to the Kufan school, which allows prepositions to substitute for each other. Next, he discusses the metaphor: the phrase 'sama' al-'aql' is an idafa (genitive construction) where the first term (sama') is the thing compared (mushabbah bihi) and the second ('aql) is the thing compared (mushabbah). The original underlying expression would be 'the intellect which is like the sky', with the common point (jami') being that both are places for the rising of 'suns' (i.e., luminaries of knowledge). The commentator then addresses the nature of the metaphor in 'hatta' (removal). He notes that calling it a pure metaphorical usage (majaz bi al-isti'ara) is not the only possibility; it could also be a transferred expression (majaz mursal) with the relation of restriction (taqyid) then generalization (itlaq): the literal physical removal (from high to low) is first freed from the restriction of physicality and applied to spiritual removal. Alternatively, one could say it is a single metaphor: the term for physical removal is transferred to the general concept of removal (whether physical or not) and then applied to spiritual removal as one of its instances. The purpose is to clarify the rhetorical device used in the line and the exact meaning of 'hatta' as a spiritual removal of veils of ignorance.

Key Terms

بدل اشتمال
بدل بعض من كل
نيابة الحروف

Metaphor Analysis: Isti'ara vs Mursal in 'Sama' al-'Aql'
Author parses the line grammatically and explains the metaphor of intellect as a sky for knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between majaz bi al-isti'ara (metaphor based on similarity) and majaz mursal (transferred expression based on other relations) in Islamic rhetorical analysis.

Core Idea
The phrase 'sama' al-'aql' is rooted in a simile: the intellect is like a sky, but rhetorical interpretations differ on whether it is a pure metaphor or a transferred expression.

The commentator examines the rhetorical nature of 'sama' al-'aql' (sky of the intellect). He reveals that the original form is a simile ('the intellect which is like the sky') due to the common point (jami') that both are places for the rising of 'suns' (knowledge and celestial bodies). Then he discusses whether the expression 'hatta' (removal) in the line is a pure metaphor (majaz bi al-isti'ara) or a transferred expression (majaz mursal) via restriction then generalization. These distinctions are central to Islamic rhetoric (balagha) and influence how we understand figurative language in religious texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying simile
The commentator says the original is 'min al-'aql alladhi huwa ka al-sama'' (from the intellect which is like the sky). Recognize that 'sama' al-'aql' is an elliptical form of a simile, where the resemblance is implicit.
The common point (jami') is that both are a place for the rising of suns (shumus) – the suns of knowledge versus physical suns.
2
Understand the two rhetorical interpretations
The commentator presents two possibilities for the metaphor in 'hatta' (removal): (1) majaz bi al-isti'ara, where the term is used non-literally based on similarity; (2) majaz mursal, where the term is transferred from a restricted sense (physical removal) to a general sense (absolute removal) and then applied spiritually.
The first is a metaphor proper; the second is a metonymy-based transfer (majaz mursal with 'alaqat al-taqyid wa al-itlaq).
3
Analyze the 'one metaphor' alternative
The commentator also suggests a third view: that there is only one metaphor – the term for physical removal is transferred to the general concept of removal, which then includes spiritual removal as an instance. This is a single metaphorical shift.
This unifies the analysis: no need for multiple steps.
4
Apply the distinction to the text
Decide which interpretation seems most plausible for 'hatta' in context. The commentator does not definitively choose one, implying all are valid. The key is to recognize the underlying rhetorical mechanism.
This flexibility is typical in balagha; different scholars may prefer different analyses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (عَنْهُمْ) أَيْ: عَنْ أَرْبَابِ الحِجَا . وَقَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ) سَمَاءِ العَقْلِ بَدَلٌ مِنَ الجَارِّ وَالمَجْرُورِ قَبْلَهُ ؛ بَدَلُ اشْتِمَالٍ، أَوْ بَدَلُ بَعْضٍ مِنْ كُلِّ، وَالأَوَّلُ أَقْرَبُ . وَ(مِنْ)) وَ مِنْ بِمَعْنَى : «عَنْ» عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الكُوفِيِّينَ ؛ مِنْ تَجْوِيزِ نِيَابَةِ بَعْضِ الحُرُوفِ عَنْ بَعْضٍ. وَأَشَارَ المُصَنِّفُ فِي شَرْحِهِ إِلَى أَنَّ إِضَافَةَ سَمَاءِ إِلَى العَقْلِ مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المُشَبَّهِ بِهِ إِلَى المُشَبِّهِ. وَالأَصْلُ: «مِنَ العَقْلِ الَّذِي هُوَ كَالسَّمَاءِ؛ بِجَامِعِ : أَنَّ كُلًّا مَحَلٌّ لِطُلُوعِ الشَّمُوسِ. قوله : (مَجَازاً بِالِاسْتِعَارَةِ هذا لا يتعيَّن بل يصح أن يكون مرسلاً لعلاقة التقييد ثم الإطلاق؛ بأن تطلق الإزاحة الحسيَّة عن التّقييد بالحسيَّة، وتستعمل فى المعنوية. ولك أن تقول : ليس هنا إلا مجاز واحدٌ؛ بأن يُنقل الحظ عن الإزاحة الحسيَّة مِن عُلْوِ إِلَى سُفْلِ إلى مطلق إزاحة حسيَّة أو لا، من علو إلى سفل أو لا، ويستعمل في المعنوية؛ لكونها فرداً من الأفراد.
His statement: ('anhum) meaning: from the people of intellect. And his statement: (min) sama' al-'aql is a substitute for the prepositional phrase before it; a substitute of inclusion, or a substitute of part from whole, and the first is closer. And (min) and min means 'from' according to the Kufan school of allowing one particle to take the place of another. And the author indicated in his commentary that the attribution of 'sky' to 'intellect' is an attribution of the thing compared to the thing compared to. The original is: 'from the intellect which is like the sky' due to the commonality that each is a place for the rising of suns. He said: (metaphorically by metaphor) this is not determined, but it is valid for it to be a transferred metaphor through the relation of restriction then generalization; in that the physical removal is freed from the restriction of being physical and is used in the spiritual sense. And you can say: there is only one metaphor here; by transferring the term for physical removal from height to depth to absolute physical removal or not, and it is used in the spiritual sense because it is an individual of the individuals.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and rhetorical analysis of the line from the text. The commentator first explains that 'anhum' refers to 'the people of intellect' (arbab al-hija). Then he parses 'min sama' al-'aql' as a substitute (badal) for the earlier prepositional phrase, with two possibilities: badal ishtimal (substitute of inclusion) or badal ba'd min kull (part for whole), preferring the former. He also notes that 'min' here can mean 'an' (from) according to the Kufan school, which allows prepositions to substitute for each other. Next, he discusses the metaphor: the phrase 'sama' al-'aql' is an idafa (genitive construction) where the first term (sama') is the thing compared (mushabbah bihi) and the second ('aql) is the thing compared (mushabbah). The original underlying expression would be 'the intellect which is like the sky', with the common point (jami') being that both are places for the rising of 'suns' (i.e., luminaries of knowledge). The commentator then addresses the nature of the metaphor in 'hatta' (removal). He notes that calling it a pure metaphorical usage (majaz bi al-isti'ara) is not the only possibility; it could also be a transferred expression (majaz mursal) with the relation of restriction (taqyid) then generalization (itlaq): the literal physical removal (from high to low) is first freed from the restriction of physicality and applied to spiritual removal. Alternatively, one could say it is a single metaphor: the term for physical removal is transferred to the general concept of removal (whether physical or not) and then applied to spiritual removal as one of its instances. The purpose is to clarify the rhetorical device used in the line and the exact meaning of 'hatta' as a spiritual removal of veils of ignorance.
